02:15:08","updatedTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","title":"L'Oreal's chief digital officer explains how the quick adoption of e-commerce saved the company's 2020 earnings","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/loreals-chief-digital-officer-explains-how-the-quick-adoption-of-e-commerce-saved-the-companys-2020-earnings-2021-4","description":"<div class=\"content-header\"><p><a href=\"https://businessinsider.com/tb/the-people-transforming-business-in-2020-9\"><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://media.tbistatic.com/bistudios/_00/dev/ING/story_banners/ING_edit_banner.png?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Transforming Business\" height=\"\" width=\"\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\"></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></a></p></div><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/607067c2a451820019b3a3a1?format=jpeg\" alt=\"GettyImages 978646004\" height=\"2000\" width=\"2666\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Lubomira Rochet speaks onstage during the Youtube session at the Cannes Lions Festival 2018 on June 19, 2018 in Cannes, France.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Richard Bord/Getty Images for Cannes Lions</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Lubomira Rochet is the chief digital officer of L&#39;Oreal.</li>\n<li>Rochet explains how the quick adoption of e-commerce saved the company&#39;s 2020 earnings amid widespread financial crisis in the retail industry.</li>\n<li>Because of her work, Insider named Rochet to our annual list of the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/tb/the-list-of-300-people-transforming-business-in-europe-2020-9\">10 leaders transforming media and advertising in Europe.</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/tb/the-people-transforming-business-in-2020-9\"><strong>Visit Insider&#39;s Transforming Business homepage for more stories</strong></a><strong>.</strong></li>\n</ul><p>As vaccination programs across the globe begin to bite into the spread of Covid-19, retail businesses are starting to think about how they&#39;re going to welcome back customers who have saved cash during the last year&#39;s crisis.</p><p>One of the sectors looking for a new path out of the crisis is the cosmetics industry. While some sectors - like medicine, household cleaners and soap, and vitamins and supplements all saw increases in purchases during the pandemic,<a href=\"https://www.jpmorgan.com/solutions/cib/research/covid-spending-habits\"> according to JP Morgan</a>, the world cut back on cosmetics.</p><p>There are several reasons for this: as nationwide lockdowns have disrupted normal life, many people have been spending less time in front of others, and when they do, masks have made it impracticle to spend the same amount of time on facial cosmetics. Another reason is that the cosmetics industry traditionally relies on tangible, in-person sales. This is why staffed cosmetics counters are a staple of many department stores.</p><p>L&#39;Oreal is one of the largest cosmetics companies in the world, and Lubomira Rochet - who made Insider&#39;s list of <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/tb/the-list-of-300-people-transforming-business-in-europe-2020-9\">100 people transforming business in Europe</a> last year - has been tasked with navigating the firm through the pandemic. Rochet is the firm&#39;s chief digital officer, and based on widespread industry trends, the last 12 months should have been a sure-fire path to decreased profits for the company. Yet L&#39;Oreal&#39;s<a href=\"https://www.loreal-finance.com/eng/news-release/2020-annual-results\"> full-year financial results</a> for 2020, published in late February, saw things staying steady.</p><p>&#34;L&#39;Oréal has traversed this crisis in the best possible condition and has even grown stronger,&#34; Jean-Paul Agon, the company&#39;s chairman and CEO, said when revealing the results. The reason? L&#39;Oreal&#39;s forward-looking bet on e-commerce sales. &#34;Thanks to its strength in digital and e-commerce, which has again increased considerably during the crisis, L&#39;Oréal has been able to maintain a close relationship with all its consumers and compensate to a large extent for the closure of points of sale,&#34; added Agon. In all, e-commerce sales rose at L&#39;Oreal by 62% in 2020, and accounted for one dollar in every four spent with the company.</p><p>The bumper results are the payoff for a decade of work. &#34;The matter of fact is L&#39;Oreal started its transformation 10 years ago which served us well when covid hit, because we were ready,&#34; Rochet told <em>Insider</em> in mid-2020. The digitialization of the operating model for the company was crucial to making sure the firm managed to weather the crisis, but it was also one that Rochet had seen as a key area long before that.</p><p>&#34;We spent a lot adapting our marketing to the digital age,&#34; Rochet said. &#34;Investing new formats and platforms from YouTube to TikTok to Instagram to WeChat, and really completely changing our formats for faster and more interactive formats. That has been quite a journey.&#34;</p><p>But it&#39;s the way that people tend to buy their makeup that has seen the most significant transformation. &#34;We have invested in technology such as AR or VR to give [customers] an extra experience when they shop our products,&#34; said Rochet. &#34;Those are things like virtual make-up or hair colour try-ons. It&#39;s about teleconsultations that were big during covid. Those are service we propose to our consumers to enrich the experience.&#34;</p><p>Like many things, the coronavirus pandemic simply accelerated existing trends that had been in train for years. Rochet points to the rise of livestreaming sales in China as an example of how the pandemic has amplified what was already there, making it more important and significant for consumers battling the challenges of coronavirus.</p><p>And as stores and businesses begin to reopen, Rochet feels L&#39;Oreal is in a position of power. &#34;We&#39;re moving to an interesting moment where more people in a low-touch economy don&#39;t want to touch products in the store,&#34; she explained. &#34;They don&#39;t want physical testers. So we&#39;re introducing services like virtual make-up try on, through a QR code people can experience the colours and the looks, but virtually.&#34;</p><p>It&#39;s something her CEO and chairman also agrees with. Setting out 2020&#39;s financial results, Agon looked forward to 2021 with positivity. &#34;Driven by the strength of its strategic choices and a determined dynamic across the year, L&#39;Oréal has adapted to this unprecedented context and terrible pandemic with speed and agility, accelerated all of its transformations and will emerge stronger,&#34; he said.</p><p>&#34;At the beginning of this new year, which remains marked by uncertainty regarding the evolution of the pandemic, but also by consumer&#39;s appetite for beauty that remains intact across the world, we are confident in our capacity to outperform the market again this year and, subject to the evolution of the sanitary crisis, achieve a year of growth in sales and profits.&#34;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Sign up here for our daily newsletter, 10 Things Before the Opening Bell.</a></strong></li>\n</ul><p>Piper Sandler&#39;s Craig W. Johnson, CFA, CMT said he believes Lumber prices could &#34;very easily&#34; move above $1,300 per thousand board feet this year even after notching a <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/lumber-prices-hit-record-highs-soaring-past-year-2021-4-1030299977\">record high</a> in April.</p><p>That figure represents a 12% increase from Tuesday&#39;s closing price of $1,154 per thousand board feet. Lumber prices have soared more than 250% in the past year alone.</p><p>In an interview with Insider, Johnson, a technical research market strategist, said that lumber prices have broken out of a &#34;huge consolidation&#34; that occurred from 2018 through 2020 and are set to soar amid the economic reopening.</p><p>Based on technical analysis, Johnson sees lumber prices continuing to rise to at least $1,300 over the next six to nine months.</p><p>The strategist noted that there is an enormous demand for housing and renovations that&#39;s pushing lumber prices higher.</p><p> Johnson said that with interest rates as low as they are, there&#39;s an overall sense that this is the lowest mortgage rate many people are ever going to get.</p><p>As a result of these <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/coinbase-100-billion-valuation-reasonable-disruptive-firm-manhattan-venture-partners-2021-4-1030304567\">low mortgage rates</a>, a new <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/wfh-expert-economist-nick-bloom-says-future-of-work-messy-2021-4\">work-from-home trend</a>, and young people <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/millennials-gen-z-leaving-cities-for-suburbs-amid-pandemic-2020-11\">moving out of cities</a>, home buyers are lapping up properties at historic rates, and that&#39;s putting pressure on lumber supply.</p><p>Johnson also said that timber companies are struggling to catch up with demand after shutting down some of their operations during the pandemic.</p><p>The technical research strategist added that, based on what he&#39;s been reading, timber companies won&#39;t be putting more sawmills in place to meet demand either because they lack the economic incentive to add capacity.</p><p>Creating more sawmills is a capital-intensive process that requires producers to get permits, and that can&#39;t be done quickly.</p><p>Johnson also said the upcoming hurricane season could exacerbate the lumber price issue by adding to demand.</p><p>When asked what could stall rising lumber prices, Johnson said that only the slowing of economic activity would cause prices to fall, but noted that right now he believes that&#39;s unlikely as we are in the &#34;great wide open&#34; for economic growth post-pandemic.</p><p>Lumber traded up 2.7%, at roughly $1,212, as of 12:04 p.m. ET on Wednesday.</p><p><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/commodities/lumber-price\">Watch it trade live here.</a></p><div class=\"read-original\">Read +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/607710fc74da0300181e1a29?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"ca143bc67f80bd2821812ea08d635d95","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn55@2020_3/2021/04/14/18-15-27-819_f6775c8a32b14668.webp","destWidth":3714,"destHeight":2785,"sourceBytes":2907622,"destBytes":2907622,"author":"wdaniel@businessinsider.com (Will Daniel)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/607710e6420615001817573b?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn41@2020_6/2021/04/14/18-15-36-191_f5f14e8f37f13cac.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn1@2020_2/2021/04/14/18-15-35-145_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=mYm7nv4dY2E:e617nHxMyTk:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn54@2020_2/2021/04/14/18-15-35-719_1d3d6d754807cd56.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=mYm7nv4dY2E:e617nHxMyTk:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn45@2020_1/2021/04/14/18-15-29-625_ddd026b44c6239bd.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn29@2020_4/2021/04/14/18-15-29-996_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=mYm7nv4dY2E:e617nHxMyTk:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn2@2020_3/2021/04/14/18-15-36-017_23fc8daf46bad692.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/mYm7nv4dY2E":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn25@2020_4/2021/04/14/18-15-35-718_7db5b17697009912.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1618424108839},{"createdTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","updatedTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","title":"Coinbase says the entire crypto market could be destabilized if Bitcoin's anonymous creator is ever revealed or sells their $30 billion stake","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/bitcoin-creators-30-billion-destabilize-crypto-market-coinbase-risks-2021-2","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6037db95bed5c50011a2c72d?format=jpeg\" alt=\"brian armstrong coinbase\" height=\"1503\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Coinbase CEO Brian Armstrong.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Christie Hemm Klok/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Coinbase will go public on the Nasdaq on Wednesday via a direct listing.</li>\n<li>In its February filing, the trading platform cites Satoshi Nakamoto&#39;s identity as a risk factor.</li>\n<li>The creator&#39;s cache of bitcoins could wreak havoc on the market if Nakamoto sold their collection.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit the Business section of Insider for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Cryptocurrency trading platform Coinbase - which has a over $100 billion valuation - said Satoshi Nakamoto could topple an over <a href=\"https://www.cnbc.com/2021/03/09/bitcoin-btc-value-exceeds-1-trillion-for-second-time.html\">$1 trillion bitcoin market.</a></p><p>Coinbase will go public on Wednesday as a direct listing on the Nasdaq. The company has been assigned a reference price of $250 per share, according to <a href=\"http://www.nasdaqtrader.com/TraderNews.aspx?id=ETA2021-28\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"60\" data-uri=\"f69b057352adc4a0d1fe8839b2f35d2c\">NasdaqTrader</a>.</p><p>In the documents the company released in February for its public debut, Coinbase said Satoshi Nakamoto - the pseudonym used by the individual or group of people who developed bitcoin - could cause significant damage to the company.</p><p>If the identity of the creator was revealed, it could cause bitcoin prices to deteriorate, according to the filing. </p><p>The filing also referenced Nakamoto&#39;s personal stash of bitcoins, which totals <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/satoshi-nakamoto-owns-one-million-bitcoin-700-price-2016-6\">over 1 million</a>. As of April, <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/bitcoin-price-all-time-high-btc-stimulus-institutional-coinbase-cryptocurrencies-2021-4-1030297319\">one bitcoin was worth over $64,000 - an all-time record.</a></p><p>Nakamoto could negatively affect Coinbase, the company said, and destabilize the entire crypto market if the creator decided to transfer his bitcoins, which are <a href=\"https://decrypt.co/34810/how-many-bitcoin-does-its-inventor-satoshi-nakamoto-still-own\">valued at over $30 billion</a>.</p><p><em><strong>Read more: </strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/what-nfts-mean-how-small-businesses-creators-make-money-nonfungible-tokens\" data-e2e-name=\"inline-read-more-link\">Mark Cuban explains how NFTs could provide new revenue streams for small businesses and creators</a></em></p><p>The creator was the first entity to ever mine for bitcoins, and Nakamoto&#39;s stake in the digital currency accounts for nearly 5% of the entire bitcoin market, as there are only <a href=\"https://www.investopedia.com/tech/what-happens-bitcoin-after-21-million-mined/\">21 million bitcoins</a> that can be mined. The entire bitcoin market is worth over $1 trillion.</p><p>Bitcoin&#39;s value has largely been driven by its deflationary tendencies. If 1.1 million bitcoins were released into the market, the digital currency&#39;s price would almost surely fall.</p><p>Similarly, Bitcoin has been praised for its decentralized nature. The currency is not beholden to any institutions or individuals. If Nakamoto was unmasked, that would place the currency under a single entity, which could discourage traders that bought into the currency for its decentralization.</p><h2>Coinbase&#39;s success is largely tied to Bitcoin&#39;s rise</h2><p>In a nod to the Bitcoin creator, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/coinbase-sent-bitcoin-creator-satoshi-nakamoto-copy-of-filing-2021-2\">Coinbase listed Nakamoto as one of the recipients of its public filing.</a> </p><p>Coinbase can attribute much of its success to Bitcoin and its creator, who in 2009 developed it as the first decentralized digital currency.</p><p>In the years since, Bitcoin has largely dominated the cryptocurrency world, rising <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/bitcoin-price-record-high-48000-elon-musk-tesla-michael-novogratz-2021-2-1030059669\">over 400% in the past year</a> alone to easily remain the largest digital coin by market cap.</p><p>Coinbase is poised to continue to benefit from the cryptocurrency&#39;s rise. The trading platform is the largest in the US and has <a href=\"https://www.stockbrokers.com/guides/best-crypto-exchanges-bitcoin-trading#:~:text=Best%20Bitcoin%20Trading%20Platform&text=Here%27s%20a%20breakdown%20of%20some,brokers%20for%20US%20crypto%20trading.&text=Founded%20in%20June%20of%202012,housing%20over%2020%20million%20users.20users.\">over 20 million users</a>.</p><p>The company&#39;s founder and CEO, Brian Armstrong, referenced the invention of Bitcoin in his <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/coinbase-ceo-brian-armstrong-founders-letter-going-public-direct-listing-2021-2\">letter that was included in the public filing in February</a>.</p><p>&#34;When I first read the Bitcoin whitepaper back in 2010, I realized this computer science breakthrough might be the key to unlock this vision of the future,&#34; Armstrong said. &#34;Cryptocurrency could provide the core tenets of economic freedom to anyone: property rights, sound money, free trade, and the ability to work how and where they want.&#34;</p><p>Nakamoto&#39;s name first came to public attention after the white paper was released. The paper outlined the principles of a decentralized peer-to-peer digital payment system. In 2011, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/bitcoin-history-cryptocurrency-satoshi-nakamoto-2017-12#wait-so-who-is-this-japanese-american-guy-named-satoshi-nakamoto-7\">the creator moved on from the system</a> but has remained a figure of public interest.</p><p>There has been much <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/bitcoin-history-cryptocurrency-satoshi-nakamoto-2017-12#wait-so-who-is-this-japanese-american-guy-named-satoshi-nakamoto-7\">speculation over the years on the creator&#39;s identity</a>. Names like the Bitcoin developer <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/bitcoin-history-cryptocurrency-satoshi-nakamoto-2017-12#wait-so-who-is-this-japanese-american-guy-named-satoshi-nakamoto-7\">Nick Szabo, the entrepreneur Craig Wright, and Tesla CEO Elon Musk have been put forward as potential creators of the currency</a>.</p><p>While it is unknown whether Nakamoto will ever choose to transfer their cache of bitcoins, it seems unlikely the creator will ever reveal their identity.</p><p>By maintaining anonymity, Nakamoto could avoid legal consequences. The untraceable nature of bitcoin has also led to its use for illegal goods and services on the dark web. In January, Treasury Secretary Janet Yellen <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/bitcoin-price-cryptocurrency-should-be-curtailed-terrorism-concerns-yellen-2021-1-1029985692\">called for more restrictions on digital currencies like bitcoin because of their use in illegal financing</a>.</p><p>The unveiling would also violate one of bitcoin&#39;s founding principles that was outlined in its white paper. If a creator was unmasked, it would pose a threat to the decentralized nature of the currency - a tenet Nakamoto put at the center of his plans for Bitcoin.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the Johnson &#38; Johnson COVID-19 vaccine, it would significantly push back its timeline for reaching a key vaccination threshold, analysts say.</p><p>According to <a href=\"https://www.airfinity.com/areas/covid-19\">Airfinity</a>, a UK-based science analytics firm, the US is on track to reach 75% coverage in the general population by July 22.</p><p>However, the projection is based on using all three approved vaccines. The <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/us-plans-pause-johnson-and-johnson-vaccine-rollout-blood-clots-report-2021-4\">decision Tuesday to pause its use</a>, citing rare blood clots, means that day will come later.</p><p>Per Airfinity figures seen by Insider, if the J&#38;J vaccine is permanently suspended, the earliest the US can hit 75% with just the Pfizer and Moderna vaccines is September 17.</p><p>The White House has argued that it still has enough doses to vaccinate all US adults - but did not comment on how it might push back the timeline.</p><p>In a statement on Tuesday, White House COVID-19 Response Coordinator Jeff Zients said the pause would <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/white-house-johnson-johnson-pause-wont-significantly-impact-vaccine-plan-2021-4\">not have a &#34;significant impact&#34;</a> on the rollout of the vaccine.</p><p>&#34;Based on actions taken by the President earlier this year, the United States has secured enough Pfizer and Moderna doses for 300 million Americans,&#34; <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/white-house-johnson-johnson-pause-wont-significantly-impact-vaccine-plan-2021-4\">he said</a>.</p><p>Zients said that there is &#34;more than enough supply to continue the current pace of vaccinations of 3 million shots per day&#34;, and to meet the President&#39;s goal of 200 million shots by May.</p><p>FDA and CDC officials are due to review the evidence around the blood clots before deciding how to proceed. While a permanent suspension is possible, officials appear to expect that the use of the vaccine will resume.</p><p>Dr. Antony Fauci, the White House chief medical advisor said he expects the suspension to last <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fauci-johnson-and-johnson-vaccine-pause-delay-how-long-2021-4\">&#34;days to weeks&#34;</a>, rather than &#34;weeks to months&#34;. This would mean the overall delay would be smaller, moving the 75% date closer to July.</p><p>According to a &#34;guesstimate&#34; from Fauci in December, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fauci-up-to-90-population-needs-vaccine-for-herd-immunity-2020-12\">70% to 90%</a> of the population need to be vaccinated to reach the all important &#34;herd immunity,&#34; a threshold above which it is more difficult for the virus to circulate. </p><p>An indefinite suspension of the J&#38;J vaccine would also lengthen the EU vaccination campaign, as the 75% threshold would be reached on December 8, rather than September 30 in the EU, according to Airfinity.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 02:15:08","updatedTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","title":"Facebook is under investigation in the EU for its massive leak of 533 million people's data - and it could face a fine in the billions","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/facebook-data-leak-eu-regulator-investigation-ireland-dpc-2021-4","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f7f32d4c655f6001969eefd?format=jpeg\" alt=\"facebook mark zuckerberg\" height=\"1333\" width=\"1777\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Facebook CEO Mark Zuckerberg appears before a House Financial Services Committee hearing on Capitol Hill in Washington, Wednesday, Oct. 23, 2019.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Andrew Harnik/AP</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>A European regulator announced that it&#39;s investigating Facebook over a leak of 533 million people&#39;s data.</li>\n<li>Ireland&#39;s Data Protection Commission will probe whether Facebook broke EU privacy laws.</li>\n<li>Facebook could face a fine of up to 4% of its $86 billion global revenue if found responsible.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Europe&#39;s leading privacy regulator is investigating whether Facebook broke the law in its handling of a leak of over 533 million people&#39;s phone numbers and personal data.</p><p>Ireland&#39;s Data Protection Commission, the body charged with overseeing Facebook&#39;s privacy compliance in the European Union, <a href=\"https://www.dataprotection.ie/en/news-media/press-releases/dpc-launches-inquiry-facebook-relation-collated-dataset-facebook-user-personal-data-made-available\">announced</a> it had opened an investigation into the social media giant on Wednesday. If Facebook is found to have violated the EU&#39;s data rules, it could face a monetary fine of up to 4% of its $86 billion global revenue.</p><p>In a <a href=\"https://www.dataprotection.ie/en/news-media/press-releases/dpc-launches-inquiry-facebook-relation-collated-dataset-facebook-user-personal-data-made-available\">statement</a>, the DPC said it believes EU data rules &#34;may have been, and/or are being, infringed in relation to Facebook Users&#39; personal data.&#34;</p><p>The personal data of over 533 million Facebook users were dumped online for free in a hacking forum earlier this month, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stolen-data-of-533-million-facebook-users-leaked-online-2021-4\">Insider first reported</a>. The data included phone numbers that users didn&#39;t make public on their Facebook profiles, which were scraped by cybercriminals in violation of Facebook&#39;s terms of service. </p><p>A Facebook spokesperson said in a statement to Insider that the company is &#34;cooperating fully&#34; with the investigation, adding that the DPC is probing a now-patched vulnerability in a Facebook tool that made it possible to gather information about a Facebook user by entering their phone number. </p><p>&#34;We are cooperating fully with the IDPC in its enquiry, which relates to features that make it easier for people to find and connect with friends on our services. These features are common to many apps and we look forward to explaining them and the protections we have put in place,&#34; the spokesperson said.</p><p>When news of the leak first broke, Facebook said the data was scraped due to a vulnerability that the company <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stolen-data-of-533-million-facebook-users-leaked-online-2021-4\">patched</a> in 2019, and <a href=\"https://about.fb.com/news/2021/04/facts-on-news-reports-about-facebook-data/\">downplayed</a> the issue as &#34;previously reported&#34; - but the company <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/facebook-data-leak-security-experts-skeptical-2021-4\">never publicly addressed the vulnerability in detail</a> until the data dump this month.</p><p>Facebook also <a href=\"https://www.reuters.com/article/us-facebook-data-leak/facebook-does-not-plan-to-notify-half-billion-users-affected-by-data-leak-idUSKBN2BU2ZY\">said</a> it does not plan to notify the hundreds of millions affected by the data breach because it&#39;s not confident that it has full knowledge of which users are affected, and because users can&#39;t take steps to fix the issue given that the data has already been published online.</p><p>The DPC investigation comes on the heels of pressure from the European Commission. Justice commissioner Didier Reynders <a href=\"https://twitter.com/dreynders/status/1381663631981617163\">said</a> on Monday that he had met with the DPC head Helen Dixon regarding the Facebook leak.</p><blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet blockquote\" data-lang=\"en\" data-cards=\"\" data-conversation=\"\">-Didier Reynders  (@dreynders) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/mims/statuses/1381663631981617163?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">April 12, 2021</a></blockquote><script async=\"\" src=\"//platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script><p>The EU investigation will probe whether Facebook had a legal obligation to notify users and European regulators when it found and fixed the vulnerability. The EU&#39;s data privacy rules, known as GDPR, require such disclosures - but the GDPR only applies to data processed after 2018, and it&#39;s not yet clear if the leaked Facebook data was scraped before the GDPR went into effect.</p><p>The DPC <a href=\"https://www.dataprotection.ie/en/news-media/press-releases/dpc-launches-inquiry-facebook-relation-collated-dataset-facebook-user-personal-data-made-available\">said</a> that it has already 02:15:08","updatedTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","title":"Republicans say Biden's Afghanistan withdrawal aids terrorists, but US intelligence has zeroed in on homegrown extremism as a 'greater immediate domestic threat'","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/gop-biden-afghanistan-withdrawal-aids-terrorism-us-intelligence-homegrown-extremism-2021-4","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/607727a742061500181757a2?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Joe Biden\" height=\"1000\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Vice President Joe Biden meets with U.S. troops in Maidan Afghanistan would make it a safe haven for terrorists.</li>\n<li>But US intelligence and law enforcement now view domestic extremism as the greatest terror threat.</li>\n<li>Data shows the threat from far-right extremist groups has surged in recent years - especially 2020.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Top Republicans are ripping into President Joe Biden&#39;s plan to withdraw all remaining troops in Afghanistan <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/biden-plan-withdraw-us-troops-afghanistan-911-report-2021-4\">by September 11,</a> claiming the pullout will turn the country into a safe haven for terrorism and pose a danger to the US. But the evidence, both in terms of recent history and US intelligence, is not on their side.  </p><p>&#34;Precipitously withdrawing US forces from Afghanistan is a grave mistake. It is a retreat in the face of an enemy,&#34; Senate Minority Leader Mitch McConnell <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/national-security/republicans-afghanistan-biden/2021/04/13/7167a2f8-9c84-11eb-b7a8-014b14aeb9e4_story.html\">said</a> on the Senate floor on Tuesday. &#34;Foreign terrorists will not leave the United States alone simply because our politicians have grown tired of taking the fight to them.&#34;</p><p>Explaining her objection to Biden&#39;s planned withdrawal, GOP Rep. Liz Cheney of Wyoming, the third-ranking House Republican, on Wednesday added, &#34;We know what happens if terrorists establish safe havens. We must ensure it doesn&#39;t happen again.&#34; Cheney labeled Biden&#39;s plan &#34;reckless and dangerous.&#34; </p><blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet blockquote\" data-lang=\"en\" data-cards=\"\" data-conversation=\"\">-The Recount  (@therecount) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/mims/statuses/1382338592899469315?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">April 14, 2021</a></blockquote><script async=\"\" src=\"//platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script><h2>The nature of the terror threat to the US has changed</h2><p>The notion that pulling US troops from Afghanistan would create a safe haven for terrorists has consistently been employed as an argument against withdrawal by hawkish lawmakers in Washington, DC, as well as military leaders. But experts have long <a href=\"https://www.latimes.com/opinion/story/2019-08-16/terrorist-safe-havens-war-afghanistan\">dismissed this</a> as <a href=\"https://foreignpolicy.com/2015/01/26/al-qaeda-islamic-state-myth-of-the-terrorist-safe-haven/\">a myth</a>, largely because the evidence points to domestic extremism being the greatest terror threat to the US. </p><p>The US invaded Afghanistan less than a month after the 9/11 attacks due to the Taliban&#39;s links to Al-Qaeda and its leader, Osama bin Laden. None of the 19 hijackers on September 11, 2001, were from Afghanistan - though some did <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/world/wp/2018/11/30/feature/this-is-the-man-who-recruited-the-9-11-hijackers/\">train there</a>.</p><p>Twenty years after the US invasion, bin Laden was killed by Navy SEALs in Pakistan. But the US has not seen a terror attack on the same scale as 9/11, and the biggest terror threats to the US are now homegrown. </p><p>Every lethal jihadist attack on US soil since 9/11 was carried out by a US citizen or legal resident, excluding one incident involving a Saudi in the US for military training, according to <a href=\"https://www.newamerica.org/in-depth/terrorism-in-america/who-are-terrorists/\">the New America think tank.</a></p><p>And in recent years, law enforcement has increasingly cited <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/us-intelligence-report-white-supremacists-traveling-abroad-to-network-2021-3\">far-right groups</a> - especially white supremacists - as the greatest extremism threat to the US. An annual Department of Homeland Security <a href=\"https://www.dhs.gov/sites/default/files/publications/2020_10_06_homeland-threat-assessment.pdf\">assessment</a> released in October 2020, for example, pointed to violent white supremacy as the &#34;most persistent and lethal threat in the homeland.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5ffe3255a8de7700187d389b?format=jpeg\" alt=\"capitol siege riot ladder\" height=\"2000\" width=\"3000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Rioters clash with police using big ladder trying to enter Capitol building through the front doors.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/rioters-clash-with-police-using-big-ladder-trying-to-enter-news-photo/1230465340?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Lev Radin/Pacific Press/LightRocket via Getty Images</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>The threat from such groups was pushed to the forefront of the nation&#39;s attention via the Capitol attack on January 6 - a fatal incident that former President Donald Trump provoked with false claims about the 2020 election and that Republicans have repeatedly downplayed. </p><p>In public testimony on the Capitol insurrection to the Senate in early March, FBI Director Christopher Wray told lawmakers &#34;there&#39;s no doubt&#34; the riot &#34;included individuals that we would call militia violent extremists and in some instances, individuals that were racially motivated violent extremists who advocate for, you know, the superiority of the white race.&#34;</p><p>&#34;When it comes to racially motivated violent extremism&#34; the number of investigations and arrests &#34;has grown significantly on my watch,&#34; Wray said, adding that January 6 was not an &#34;isolated event.&#34; </p><p>&#34;The number of arrests, for example, of racially motivated violent extremists, who are what you would categorize as white supremacists, last year was almost triple the number it was in my first year as director,&#34; Wray said. &#34;The problem of domestic terrorism has been metastasizing across the country for a long time now and it is not going is a way any time soon.&#34; </p><p>Domestic terrorism incidents in the US have reached the highest levels seen in 25 years, and the surge has largely been driven by white supremacists, according to data compiled by the Center for Strategic and International Studies (CSIS) and analyzed by <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/investigations/interactive/2021/domestic-terrorism-data/\">The Washington Post</a>. In 2020 alone, there were 73 domestic terrorism incidents involving far right extremists - an all-time high in CSIS&#39;s database. Meanwhile, there were 25 left-wing attacks last year. </p><p>The data showed right-wing extremists have been involved in 267 plots or attacks since 2015, leading to 91 fatalities. During the same period, 66 incidents and 19 fatalities were attributed to far-left extremists. </p><h2>Biden is not willing to wait for the &#39;ideal&#39; conditions for withdrawal, because they&#39;ll never come</h2><p>US intelligence agencies do not believe that Al Qaeda and other terrorist organizations pose an imminent threat to strike the US from Afghanistan, <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/04/13/us/politics/afghanistan-terrorism-threat.html\">according to The New York Times</a>, even as a major <a href=\"https://int.nyt.com/data/documenttools/annual-threat-assessment-report/5bd104278cd017bd/full.pdf\">annual intelligence report</a> released Tuesday said that the Taliban is &#34;likely to make gains on the battlefield, and the Afghan Government will struggle to hold the Taliban at bay if the coalition withdraws support.&#34;</p><p>The intelligence report also said that while groups like ISIS and Al Qaeda continue to &#34;seek to conduct attacks inside the United States&#34; and pose a danger to US interests overseas, it underscored that &#34;US-based lone actors and small cells with a broad range of ideological motivations pose a greater immediate domestic threat.&#34;</p><p>The report, which focuses on worldwide threats to the national security of the US, went on to say that domestic violent extremists &#34;motivated by a range of ideologies that are not connected to or inspired by&#34; jihadist terrorist organizations like Al Qaeda and ISIS &#34;pose an elevated threat to the United States.&#34;  </p><p>That said, CIA Director William Burns on Wednesday said there&#39;s a &#34;significant risk&#34; that terrorist groups in Afghanistan - like Al Qaeda - will seek to rebuild after a US withdrawal, and it&#39;ll be harder for the US to gather intelligence and act on threats. But Burns also said that Al Qaeda and ISIS currently lack the ability to attack the homeland and aren&#39;t the most dangerous transnational terror groups anymore. &#34;There are terrorist groups that represent much more serious threats,&#34; Burns told the Senate Intelligence Committee.</p><p>Along these lines, Biden has apparently concluded that while there are risks in withdrawing, they&#39;re relatively small and the costs of remaining in Afghanistan would be far greater. </p><p>As <a href=\"https://twitter.com/msnbc/status/1382364034020823042?s=21\">Biden was expected to say</a> in remarks on the planned withdrawal on Wednesday, the US &#34;cannot continue the cycle of extending or expanding our military presence in Afghanistan hoping to create the ideal conditions for our withdrawal, expecting a different result.&#34;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a href='https://www.businessinsider.com/gop-biden-afghanistan-withdrawal-aids-terrorism-us-intelligence-homegrown-extremism-2021-4'>Business Insider</a></div><div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a Haltiwanger)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/607727a742061500181757a2?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn73@2020_4/2021/04/14/18-15-35-881_91b2e457476e5b73.webp","https://i.insider.com/5ffe3255a8de7700187d389b?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn22@2020_5/2021/04/14/18-15-35-531_a55e1d92fa875fec.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn1@2020_2/2021/04/14/18-15-35-145_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=KostNJR1kVM:zD6_mkuA9ks:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn97@2020_1/2021/04/14/18-15-35-205_2919f93a93e7a980.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=KostNJR1kVM:zD6_mkuA9ks:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn55@2020_4/2021/04/14/18-15-28-171_7fa58ba0ffe020f0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn29@2020_4/2021/04/14/18-15-29-996_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=KostNJR1kVM:zD6_mkuA9ks:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn38@2020_6/2021/04/14/18-15-35-673_63b3c330865a7a83.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/KostNJR1kVM":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn21@2020_3/2021/04/14/18-15-36-013_1c55444cb3431b7e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1618424108841},{"createdTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","updatedTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","title":"The Biden administration reportedly spent months preparing for an inflation spike that hasn't come yet - and it's still worried","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/biden-stimulus-inflation-risk-modeling-treasury-department-federal-reserve-infrastructure-2021-4","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6076f00974da0300181e191d?format=jpeg\" alt=\"President Joe Biden and Janet Yellen White House.JPG\" height=\"2957\" width=\"5914\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>President Joe Biden meets with Treasury Secretary Janet Yellen in the Oval Office at the White House in Washington, U.S., January 29, 2021.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Kevin Lamarque/Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>White House and Treasury officials spent months testing inflation scenarios, the <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/04/13/business/economy/biden-inflation-stimulus.html\">NYT reports</a>.</li>\n<li>No scenario showed inflation rising so quickly that the Fed would lose control of price growth.</li>\n<li>The findings open the door for Biden to spend trillions more on infrastructure and social care.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>The Biden administration spent much of its first days in office testing how further stimulus might drive inflation higher. No modeled scenario saw price growth surge out of control, <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/04/13/business/economy/biden-inflation-stimulus.html\">The New York Times</a> reported on Wednesday.</p><p>Still, the report said repeatedly that White House and Treasury officials are &#34;worried&#34; about the issue.</p><p>The inflation debate has loomed large over the White House since before President Joe Biden was even inaugurated. The president <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/joe-biden-stimulus-plan-details-checks-congress-direct-payments-unemployment-2021-1\">unveiled a $1.9 trillion relief proposal</a> in January, pitching the plan as an additional boost for the US economic recovery. Largely Democrat-affiliated economists have <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/inflation-stimulus-debate-economic-growth-spending-federal-reserve-larry-summers-2021-3\">fiercely debated the inflation risks</a> of such large deficit-financed spending, led by former Obama- and Clinton-administration official Larry Summers.</p><p>Democrats largely backed the measure, saying the risks of retracting government support were greater than the risks of spending too much. But Republicans - and even some moderate Democrats - balked at the hefty price tag and cited fears that another set of stimulus checks could spark a dangerous surge in inflation.</p><p>&#34;This is the least responsible fiscal macroeconomic policy we&#39;ve had for the last 40 years,&#34; Summers said in a March interview with <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2021-03-20/summers-says-u-s-facing-worst-macroeconomic-policy-in-40-years\">Bloomberg TV</a>, adding the measures are a product of &#34;intransigence&#34; among Democrats and &#34;irresponsible behavior&#34; among Republicans.</p><p>Democrats went ahead without any Republican votes, passing the bill via reconciliation, and Biden signed it into law on March 11. Still, the stimulus push wasn&#39;t without some trepidation. A handful of officials in the Treasury Department spent several months modeling how Americans would deploy new fiscal support, and whether any outcome could lead to stifling inflation, according to The Times. Treasury Secretary and former Federal Reserve Chair Janet Yellen even helped create the models.</p><p>Their observations were encouraging and lend new support to Biden&#39;s latest spending proposal. The team tested a range of potentialities for how quickly Americans would spend stimulus, where they would deploy cash, and how the labor market&#39;s recovery would affect inflation. Yet no outcome saw inflation charge out of the Fed&#39;s control and risk a new recession, the Times reported.</p><p>The findings have been hinted at in statements from the White House and the Treasury in recent weeks. Long-term scarring in the labor market poses a greater risk than inflation, Yellen told ABC&#39;s &#34;This Week&#34; in March. Economic reopening is expected to drive a jump in prices, but the effects will likely be temporary and fail to drive sustained inflation, she added.</p><p>The administration&#39;s Council of Economic Advisors mirrored Yellen in a Monday <a href=\"https://www.whitehouse.gov/briefing-room/blog/2021/04/12/pandemic-prices-assessing-inflation-in-the-months-and-years-ahead/\">blog post</a>. A temporary rise in inflation is consistent with trends seen after other major events like wars or past labor-market rebounds, economists Ernie Tedeschi and Jared Bernstein said. The White House will continue to monitor consumer prices, but it expects inflation to fade as actual price growth &#34;runs more in line with longer-run expectations,&#34; they added.</p><p>Fed Chair Jerome Powell has repeatedly backed up such an outlook. The central bank chief said last month that the Fed will &#34;<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fed-jerome-powell-inflation-patient-stimulus-treasury-yields-reopening-monetarypolicy-2021-3\">be patient</a>&#34; in monitoring inflation and eventually lifting interest rates. The most likely scenario during the recovery is that prices move higher but fail to stay elevated as the country enters a new sense of normalcy, Powell said in early March.</p><p>Although the Fed operates independently from the executive branch and doesn&#39;t play a role in fiscal spending, officials testing inflation scenarios told the Times that the Biden administration trusts the Fed to intervene and stave off price growth should it accelerate faster than expected.</p><p>The latest data signals the country is far from any sort of inflation scare. The Consumer Price Index - a popular gauge of overall inflation - <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/inflation-consumer-price-index-march-economic-reopening-federal-reserve-powell-2021-4\">rose 0.6% in March</a> as stimulus, reopening, and vaccination fueled stronger economic activity. Economists expected a 0.5% gain.</p><p>Consumer prices rose 2.6% year-over-year, also exceeding estimates. The measure is skewed somewhat by year-ago data, since prices initially dropped when the pandemic first slammed the US economy. Those readings present a lower bar for year-over-year inflation. Though the data points to stronger inflation, price growth still has a ways to go before it trends at the Fed&#39;s above-2% level and warrants serious concern.</p><p>That opening paves the way for additional spending. Biden unveiled a $2.3 trillion infrastructure proposal late last month that includes funds for nationwide broadband, improved roads and bridges, and affordable housing. The package is expected to be spent over eight years, compared to the weeks-long rollout seen with much of Biden&#39;s stimulus plan. Such long-term deployment would present little inflationary risk, and Biden has portrayed the plan as an investment in American industry, jobs, and research as opposed to an emergency relief measure.</p><p>The March uptick in inflation, however, does signal that price growth is trending higher. Future CPI readings are set to be closely watched releases as the administration balances its spending goals with a red-hot economy. Economists and officials are anticipating stronger inflation. How price growth trends from there will determine whether the Biden administration was successful or created new risks.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a src=\"https://i.insider.com/6076ed5774da0300181e1906?format=jpeg\" alt=\"brian armstrong coinbase\" height=\"1996\" width=\"2662\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Coinbase Founder and CEO Brian Armstrong attends Consensus 2019 at the Hilton Midtown on May 15, 2019 in New York City.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Steven Ferdman/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Coinbase CEO Brian Armstrong said regulation is &#34;right up there with cybersecurity&#34; in crypto risks.</li>\n<li>Coinbase is about to be the first and largest cryptocurrency firm to go public.</li>\n<li>But as Coinbase makes it market debut, Armstrong said scrutiny of the company may mount.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Coinbase CEO Brian Armstrong said two of the biggest risks to cryptocurrency are cybersecurity and regulation.</p><p>The executive spoke with <a href=\"https://www.cnbc.com/2021/04/14/coinbase-ceo-says-regulation-is-one-of-the-biggest-threats-to-crypto.html\">CNBC</a> on Wednesday ahead of his company&#39;s public debut via a direct listing. Coinbase is the first major cryptocurrency company to go public, representing the industry&#39;s booming growth. </p><p>But as the company moves into the public market, Armstrong noted that scrutiny of Coinbase may also mount as people seek to understand the burgeoning business. And because of that, regulation of the industry poses one of the biggest risks to the market, a risk that&#39;s &#34;right up there with cybersecurity.&#34; <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/coinbase-s-1-risk-factors-bitcoin-volatility-cyber-attacks-regulation-2021-2#3-as-a-company-built-around-an-online-product-coindesk-is-particularly-vulnerable-to-cyber-attacks-3\">In its S-1 filing,</a> the company explicitly listed cyber attacks as a risk factor for the company, given that Coinbase is an online platform that manages a digital currency.</p><p>&#34;We&#39;re very excited and happy to play by the rules,&#34; Armstrong told CNBC, regarding potential regulation. &#34;And basically, we just ask that, hey, we want to be treated on those level playing field with traditional financial services at the very least and not have any kind of punishment for being in the crypto space.&#34;</p><p><em><strong>Read more: </strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/how-coinbase-ceo-brian-armstrong-landed-a-100-million-term-sheet-2021-4\" data-e2e-name=\"inline-read-more-link\">A phone call from Coinbase CEO Brian Armstrong the night before a $100 million deal left one VC feeling &#39;nauseous.&#39;</a></em></p><p>The exec added that Coinbase is &#34;very happy to engage&#34; with US lawmakers on &#34;how we can most thoughtfully build this industry and this company.&#34; You can watch Armstrong&#39;s full interview with CNBC <a href=\"https://www.cnbc.com/video/2021/04/14/coinbase-ceo-brian-armstrong-on-risks-of-potential-regulation.html\">here.</a></p><p>Coinbase will begin trading on the Nasdaq under the ticker &#34;COIN&#34; <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/coinbase-plans-direct-listing-2-weeks-crypto-exchange-go-public-2021-4\">in a direct listing</a> and has set a reference price of <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/coinbase-reference-price-set-at-250-per-share-ahead-of-nasdaq-debut-2021-4\">$250 per share.</a> That is set to value the company at over <a href=\"https://www.marketwatch.com/story/coinbase-ipo-everything-you-need-to-know-about-the-watershed-moment-in-crypto-11618350086\">$65 billion</a> ahead of its public market debut.</p><p>Cryptocurrency has soared in popularity in recent years. The virtual currency is not issued or processed by the government but is instead managed on so-called blockchains, or encrypted databases. Coinbase is an exchange platform that allows users to manage that digital currency.</p><p>Bitcoin remains the most popular form of cryptocurrency - <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/bitcoin-trillion-market-cap-milestone-faster-amazon-apple-microsoft-google-2021-4-1030302376\">its price soared above $64,000 on Wednesday</a> in the lead-up to Coinbase&#39;s public debut, skyrocketing past a $1 trillion market capitalization. It only took Bitcoin 12 years to hit that milestone, which is three times faster than it took Apple to do so.</p><p>The cryptocurrency hit record highs in March, a boom that some experts have said was largely driven by stimulus checks. <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/news/bitcoin-price-record-high-coinbase-direct-listing-btc-crypto-2021-4-1030302632\">Institutional investors like Tesla and JP Morgan</a> have also gotten involved, boosting Bitcoin&#39;s success over the past few months.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 02:15:08","updatedTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","title":"Experts lay out how the chaos caused by pandemic-era panic buying could revolutionize our global supply chain","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/supply-chain-pandemic-panic-buying-demand-dhl-orderly-alom-technologies-2021-3","description":"<div class=\"content-header\"><p><a href=\"https://businessinsider.com/tb/the-people-transforming-business-in-2020-9\"><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://media.tbistatic.com/bistudios/_00/dev/ING/story_banners/ING_edit_banner.png?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Transforming Business\" height=\"\" width=\"\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\"></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></a></p></div><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5fc138b4037cbd0018612a0c?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Stockpiling toilet paper\" height=\"2699\" width=\"3994\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>In the US, one of the earliest stories of the pandemic was the toilet paper shortage.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Kathrin Ziegler/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>The COVID-19 pandemic highlighted the global nature of our supply chain - and its fragility.</li>\n<li>In a roundtable with Insider, experts discussed the revolutionary lessons learned from that period.</li>\n<li>Read the full transcript with Hannah Kain (ALOM Technologies), Mei Yee Pang (DHL), and Peter Evans (Orderly) <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/supply-chain-leaders-sustainable-e-commerce-order-packaging-toilet-paper-2021-2\">here.</a></li>\n<li>Because of their work, Insider named the panelists to our annual list of the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/tb/the-list-of-300-people-transforming-business-in-north-america-2020-9\">10 leaders transforming supply chain.</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/tb/the-people-transforming-business-in-2020-9\"><strong>Visit Insider&#39;s Transforming Business homepage for more stories</strong></a><strong>.</strong></li>\n</ul><p>A year-long struggle with the COVID-19 pandemic has brought more headlines than we as a society - as well as the people writing those headlines - can really handle. There&#39;s been speculation, sadness, chaos, fear, isolation, data, and graphs. So many graphs.</p><p>But one of the earliest headlines of the pandemic wasn&#39;t about any of that. It was about supply, and the supply of one product in particular: toilet paper. </p><p>When Insider mentioned the great toilet paper crisis of 2020 in a <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/supply-chain-leaders-sustainable-e-commerce-order-packaging-toilet-paper-2021-2\">virtual roundtable</a> with Hannah Kain, founder and CEO of California-headquartered supply chain management supplier ALOM Technologies, she laughed. </p><p>&#34;Yes, we can talk about the toilet paper,&#34; she said. &#34;I never thought I would be interviewed so much about toilet paper.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5ea32cb10fc6397c8f78a964?format=jpeg\" alt=\"clorox lysol store shelves\" height=\"1375\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A shopper at a Target store in Brooklyn reaches for disinfectant wipes in March 2020.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Associated Press/Bebeto Matthews</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>When much of the US shifted to quarantine overnight last March, the world&#39;s supply chain was forced into the spotlight. Suddenly, we were ordering more online: our groceries, our home office setups, our bread ingredients, our puzzles. When we did face this new, mysterious virus to go into a store, we were met with empty shelves and freshly printed signs telling us we couldn&#39;t buy more than two jugs of milk. Sales of toilet paper in the US <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/why-toilet-paper-demand-spiked-845-how-companies-kept-up-2020-5\">shot up 845%</a>, demand for Clorox products <a href=\"https://www.nbcnews.com/business/business-news/demand-clorox-products-500-percent-when-can-we-expect-see-n1250868\">spiked by 500%</a>, and treadmill sales <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/road-to-recovery/2021/01/07/home-fitness-boom/\">more than doubled</a>.</p><p>As we depended on our supply chain more, we criticized it more. After all, how hard could it be to just make some more toilet paper?</p><p>As consumers, we focused on the supply side of the equation. But Kain said for the professionals, supply problems haven&#39;t been the story for much of the pandemic - demand shift has. </p><p>&#34;Demand shifted so dramatically, sometimes 50% or 100% compared to forecast,&#34; Kain said. &#34;The supply really got constrained, right? If the demand had not shifted, the supply side would have been difficult, but it would still have been flowing really, really well.</p><p>&#34;Many [journalists] say, &#39;But why don&#39;t they just make more?&#39; I&#39;m like, &#39;Well, guess what, you need equipment to make more toilet tissue, and where do you get the equipment from?&#39; A lot of times, from China. But even if you use a local equipment maker, they need spare parts from all over the world and it just takes time to deploy it.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f08b5ca4dca6827d46b2106?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Toilet paper production\" height=\"1080\" width=\"1920\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The US saw demand spikes as the pandemic took hold, and toilet paper sales shot up 845%.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Kain said demand changed in two important ways: which products people bought and, as people shifted even more of their purchasing online, which channels they bought them through.</p><p>Mei Yee Pang, the Singapore-based head of DHL Asia Pacific Innovation, saw similar patterns, illustrating a common theme of the pandemic: how intertwined supply chains are, for better and for worse. </p><p>&#34;Supply chains today are so global, you see pretty much the same phenomena everywhere we go,&#34; Pang told Insider. &#34;We too had our toilet paper issues. There was, at some point in time, a global shortage of glassware because everybody started making jams at home and needed glass jars.</p><p>&#34;So we do see very interesting demand shapes, and a lot of them, looking back, are something that we can expect. That&#39;s where big data potentially in the future can come in more, better forecasting some of these effects that we normally wouldn&#39;t have expected.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f19f697988ee365d85aabe5?format=jpeg\" alt=\"costco cart toilet paper kirkland\" height=\"3024\" width=\"4032\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The production of toilet paper came into the spotlight during the shortages, with many asking why companies couldn&#39;t just make more. Experts told Insider it wasn&#39;t that easy.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Shutterstock</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Changing demand isn&#39;t the only issue the pandemic highlighted. More online ordering didn&#39;t just mean more convenience for the buyer - it meant more waste, too.</p><p>&#34;Every time I receive a parcel, I feel bad about it because I&#39;m contributing to waste,&#34; Pang said. &#34;I think this is something that needs to change. We can&#39;t be sending individual shipments using partially utilized vehicles to send stuff around to individual homes.</p><p>&#34;It&#39;s not easy, but I think as we go more and more from a less than 20% e-commerce channel to now, some companies are having that switch around to e-commerce as a major channel, we are going to see a lot of waste coming into play and it&#39;s not sustainable.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f120a094dca684e5a7e0e93?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Cargo planes\" height=\"1660\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>As many places shifted to quarantine, consumers&#39; online ordering habits expanded.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Oed/ullstein bild/Getty</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Kain thinks the switch to e-commerce is here to stay, but said there&#39;s &#34;no easy solution&#34; to making the change more sustainable from a packaging perspective. </p><p>&#34;If we go to things like consumer electronics, everything was packaged for the retail shelves,&#34; Kain said. &#34;Maybe now you have a shippable box, and you don&#39;t have a box inside a box.</p><p>&#34;We are a little bit in a tough spot right now because recycling and return packaging is of course a big issue because of the risk of infection. I think this is something that we&#39;ve got to develop over time, but I do think that the big carriers are going to come out with support in this area.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5fea2cd7a64488001819386e?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Mailed packages on front porch\" height=\"1414\" width=\"2121\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A stack of delivered packages and boxes sit outside a front door.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jennifer J Taylor/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Pang, whose employer DHL is one of those big carriers with <a href=\"https://www.dhl.com/content/g0/en/about_us/company_portrait.html#:~:text=DHL%20is%20present%20in%20over,infinite%20number%20of%20logistics%20needs.\">more than</a> 350,000 employees worldwide, said analytics will help. </p><p>&#34;In our organization, we start looking at how we can help our customers look at packaging and use data information to optimize the way we pack, the way we pelletize,&#34; Pang said. &#34;Every small bit counts to really reducing our footprint, and at the same time, lowers cost. So what&#39;s not to like about this sort of solution?&#34;</p><p>Peter Evans, CEO of the UK-based sustainable supply chain technology company Orderly, told Insider his company&#39;s main product is something called a &#34;scorecard.&#34; Its goals include <a href=\"https://www.derby.ac.uk/business-services/funding-services-and-support/access-to-funding/case-studies/orderly---technology/\">reducing waste of both products and packaging</a>.</p><p>&#34;It rates everyone who manages supply chain operations, from someone in a warehouse to the CEO, on a scale of zero to five,&#34; Evans said. &#34;Zero being &#39;You&#39;re wrecking the planet and you&#39;re wrecking your business,&#39; five being &#39;You&#39;re really making some decent change here.&#39; We use AI against all this data we pull in to give each person two recommendations each week on what they can do to provide the biggest, most sustainable impact to their supply chain.</p><p>&#34;For us, it&#39;s changing people&#39;s mindsets on an individual level, showing them what can be done in the world as well - what can create the biggest benefit.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5fd7a64fe00bce00188bb33b?format=jpeg\" alt=\"ups employee packages delivery\" height=\"681\" width=\"1024\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A UPS employee delivers packages.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Noam Galai/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Kain said that&#39;s important, both in terms of sustainability and social responsibility, as more people think about their relationships with companies.</p><p>&#34;Corporate social responsibility has become way more important for decision makers,&#34; Kain said. &#34;It&#39;s driven by consumers. We want companies to be in sync with our values. We don&#39;t want them to be out of sync.</p><p>&#34;For instance, early in the pandemic, there was a survey done in the US showing 87% of consumers did not want to buy product from companies that did not keep their workers safe. We&#39;ve never seen sentiments like this before, and it&#39;s very healthy and good. It&#39;s forcing the corporations to think differently about their supply chain in a very healthy manner. In any crisis, there&#39;s a silver lining, and I think that&#39;s it.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5fbd2b8f32f2170011f70e16?format=jpeg\" alt=\"amazon package logo warehouse\" height=\"1333\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A worker loads a truck with packages at an Amazon packaging center in Germany.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Sean Gallup/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Another silver lining might be that when or if this kind of global crisis happens again, the supply chain will be a bit more prepared for what&#39;s coming - thanks, in part, to how big of a spotlight its struggles received when the pandemic hit.</p><p>&#34;I think there&#39;s a newfound respect for the sector, from the boardroom to the individual consumers at home,&#34; Pang said. &#34;There&#39;s a newfound priority placed on the sector to put in more technology, to put in more innovation, to put in more R&#38;D into making it more agile and more prepared for situations like this, so I&#39;m quite optimistic about what we can see from the sector in the next few years.&#34;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a CEO Mark Tritton.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Courtesy of Bed Bath &#38; Beyond</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Bed Bath &#38; Beyond shares lost as much as 15% on Wednesday page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/bbby-stock\">Bed Bath &#38; Beyond shares</a> were knocked sharply lower Wednesday after first-quarter sales results from the housewares retailer fell short of Wall Street&#39;s target.</p><p>The company on Wednesday posted quarterly adjusted earnings of $0.40 per share, higher than the analyst consensus estimate of $0.41 per share from Refinitiv and up from $0.38 per share a year earlier.</p><p>Sales for the quarter ended Feb. 29 fell by 16% to $2.62 billion from $3.11 billion a year ago, slightly missing the $2.63 billion that Wall Street had anticipated. </p><p>Shares fell as much as 15% to $23.68 in heavy volume before the losses were pared to 10% during the session. The company&#39;s stock has soared over the past year from about $4 each.</p><p>Bed Bath &#38; Beyond, which is executing a turnaround plan, said quarterly sales were hurt in part by divestitures and permanent store closures. Bed Bath &#38; Beyond in January sold Cost Plus World Market to private equity firm Kingswood Capital Management and in November completed the sale of Christmas Tree Shops and its institutional Linen Holdings business.</p><p>First-quarter comparable store sales decreased 20%, the company said. Total enterprise same-store sales rose by 4% while online sales surged by 86%. The company said its strongest categories during the period included bedding, bath and kitchen food preparation.</p><p>The company reaffirmed its fiscal 2021 outlook for net sales of $8 billion to $8.2 billion and its adjusted EBITDA guidance of $500 billion to $525 million.</p><p>&#34;As our transformation continues to take hold, we will show up differently for our customers with enhanced omnichannel experiences and modern stores,&#34; among other actions, said Mark Tritton, Bed Bath &#38; Beyond&#39;s president and CEO, in the earnings statement. </p><div class=\"read-original\">Read 02:15:08","updatedTime":"2021-04-15 02:15:08","title":"Watch an interview with the last living witness to the Lincoln assassination","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/interview-with-the-last-living-witness-of-the-lincoln-assassination-2019-7","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5d1a38dfa17d6c08b50b3282?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Abraham Lincoln Ford&#39;s Theater John Wilkes Booth assassination\" height=\"700\" width=\"1400\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>An illustration of John Wilkes Booth preparing to shoot President Abraham Lincoln at Ford&#39;s Theater, April 14, 1865.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Lincoln_assassination_slide_c1900_-_Restoration.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Wikimedia Commons</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>President Abraham Lincoln was assassinated on the night of April 14, 1865.</li>\n<li> Samuel J. Seymour, then just 5 years old, was among the witnesses inside Ford&#39;s Theater.</li>\n<li>Ninety-one years later, Seymour recounted his experience that night to a TV audience.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Samuel J. Seymour was away from his home for the first time at just 5 years old. He was with his father on a business trip to Washington, DC, a city filled to the brim with soldiers and other men with guns. He was nervous and scared at the sight of so many firearms.</p><p>To put him at ease, his nurse decided to take him to a play, and President Abraham Lincoln himself would be there.</p><p>It was an event he would never forget, as he recounted it to a TV audience and celebrity contestants Bill Cullen, Jayne Meadows, Henry Morgan, and Lucille Ball some 90-plus years later.</p><iframe width=\"840\" height=\"526\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/1RPoymt3Jx4\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"\"></iframe><p>&#34;It wasn&#39;t a pleasant thing,&#34; Seymour told Meadows when describing his night at Ford&#39;s Theater on a 1956 episode of &#34;I&#39;ve Got A Secret.&#34; &#34;I was scared to death.&#34;</p><p>When Lincoln arrived, he smiled and greeted the crowd from a flag-draped booth in the balcony. The President&#39;s smile and the mood of the theater relaxed the young boy. Until a shot rang out. Strangely, the 5-year-old Seymour was very concerned about the man who appeared to have fallen from the balcony of the theater in the middle of the performance. He had no idea someone had been shot, let alone that it was President Lincoln.</p><p>&#34;Pandemonium&#34; then swept through the theater, Seymour recalled, as his nurse hurried the boy out of the theater. He heard calls of &#34;Lincoln&#39;s shot! The President is dead!&#34;</p><p>The man, of course, was Lincoln&#39;s assassin, John Wilkes Booth. Booth waited until the play&#39;s funniest line when the shot would be masked by the sound of laughter. Booth calmly walked into the President&#39;s booth, barred the door, and fired a single shot into the President, who was laughing at the line.</p><p>Union Army Maj. Henry Rathbone, who accompanied Lincoln that night with their wives, fought Booth for his single-shot derringer and was stabbed for his effort. His constant wrangling with Booth caused the assassin&#39;s boot spur to get tangled in the flag as he jumped from the President&#39;s box. This is why Booth landed awkwardly on his leg.</p><p>Many in the crowd were confused. Not everyone heard the shot, and many thought it was still part of the play. Little Samuel Seymour didn&#39;t understand it either.</p><p>&#34;I saw Lincoln slumped forward in his seat,&#34; the old man said. &#34;That night I was shot 50 times, at least, in my dreams - and I sometimes relive the horror of Lincoln&#39;s assassination, dozing in my rocker as an old codger like me is bound to do.&#34;</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a
